<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Emissions trading</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>

</tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Green_economics_sidebar" title="Template:Green economics sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Green_economics_sidebar" title="Template talk:Green economics sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Green_economics_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Emissions trading</b>, or <b>cap and trade</b>, is a market-based approach to controlling <a href="/wiki/Pollution" title="Pollution">pollution</a> by providing <a href="/wiki/Economics" title="Economics">economic</a> <a href="/wiki/Incentive" title="Incentive">incentives</a> for achieving reductions in the emissions of <a href="/wiki/Pollutant" title="Pollutant">pollutants</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stavins01_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-stavins01-1">[1]</a></sup> In contrast to command-and-control environmental regulations such as <a href="/wiki/Best_available_technology" title="Best available technology">best available technology</a> (BAT) standards and government subsidies, cap and trade (CAT) programs are a type of flexible environmental regulation<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-2">[2]</a></sup> that allows organizations to decide how best to meet policy targets. Various countries, states and groups of companies have adopted such trading systems, notably for mitigating <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>A central authority (usually a <a href="/wiki/Government_agency" title="Government agency">governmental</a> body) allocates or sells a limited number of permits to discharge specific quantities of a specific pollutant per time period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-glossary_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-glossary-4">[4]</a></sup> Polluters are required to hold permits in amount equal to their emissions. Polluters that want to increase their emissions must buy permits from others willing to sell them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stavins01_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-stavins01-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Financial_derivatives" title="Financial derivatives">Financial derivatives</a> of permits can also be traded on secondary markets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-parlinfo.aph.gov.au_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-parlinfo.aph.gov.au-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>In theory, polluters who can reduce emissions most cheaply will do so, achieving the emission reduction at the lowest cost to society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Montgomery1972_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Montgomery1972-10">[10]</a></sup>  Cap and trade is meant to provide the private sector with the flexibility required to reduce emissions while stimulating technological innovation and economic growth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are active trading programs in several <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Air_pollutant" title="Air pollutant">air pollutants</a>. For <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gases" title="Greenhouse gases">greenhouse gases</a>, which cause climate change, permit units are often called <i><a href="/wiki/Carbon_credit" title="Carbon credit">carbon credits</a></i>. The largest greenhouse gases trading program is the <a href="/wiki/European_Union_Emission_Trading_Scheme" title="European Union Emission Trading Scheme">European Union Emission Trading Scheme</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> which trades primarily in <i>European Union Allowances</i> (<i>EUAs</i>); the Californian scheme trades in California Carbon Allowances, the New Zealand scheme in New Zealand Units and the Australian scheme in Australian Units.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-parlinfo.aph.gov.au_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-parlinfo.aph.gov.au-9">[9]</a></sup> The United States has a <a href="/wiki/Acid_Rain_Program" title="Acid Rain Program">national market</a> to reduce <a href="/wiki/Acid_rain" title="Acid rain">acid rain</a> and several regional markets in <a href="/wiki/NOx" title="NOx">nitrogen oxides</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">Overview</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emissions_trading&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Overview">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coal_power_plant_Datteln_2_Crop1.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2145" data-file-width="2325" height="185" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Coal_power_plant_Datteln_2_Crop1.png/200px-Coal_power_plant_Datteln_2_Crop1.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Coal_power_plant_Datteln_2_Crop1.png/300px-Coal_power_plant_Datteln_2_Crop1.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Coal_power_plant_Datteln_2_Crop1.png/400px-Coal_power_plant_Datteln_2_Crop1.png 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Coal_power_plant_Datteln_2_Crop1.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A coal power plant in Germany. Due to emissions trading, <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a> may become a less competitive fuel than other options.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Pollution" title="Pollution">Pollution</a> is the prime example of a market externality. An <a href="/wiki/Externality" title="Externality">externality</a> is an effect of some activity on an entity (such as a person) that is not party to a market transaction related to that activity. Emissions trading is a market-based approach to address pollution. The overall goal of an emissions trading plan is to minimize the cost of meeting a set emissions target.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cap101_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-cap101-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>In an emissions trading system, the government sets an overall limit on emissions, and defines permits (also called allowances), or limited authorizations to emit, up to the level of the overall limit. The government may sell the permits, but in many existing schemes, it gives permits to participants (regulated polluters) equal to each participant's baseline emissions. The baseline is determined by reference to the participant's historical emissions. To demonstrate compliance, a participant must hold permits at least equal to the quantity of pollution it actually emitted during the time period. If every participant complies, the total pollution emitted will be at most equal to the sum of individual limits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-boswall_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-boswall-15">[15]</a></sup> Because permits can be bought and sold, a participant can choose either to use its permits exactly (by reducing its own emissions); or to emit less than its permits, and perhaps sell the excess permits; or to emit more than its permits, and buy permits from other participants. In effect, the buyer pays a charge for polluting, while the seller gains a reward for having reduced emissions.
</p><p>In many schemes, organizations which do not pollute (and therefore have no obligations) may also trade permits and financial derivatives of permits. In some schemes, participants can bank allowances to use in future periods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> In some schemes, a proportion of all traded permits must be retired periodically, causing a net reduction in emissions over time. Thus, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environmental_group" title="Environmental group">environmental groups</a> may buy and retire permits, driving up the price of the remaining permits according to the <a href="/wiki/Law_of_demand" title="Law of demand">law of demand</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> In most schemes, permit owners can donate permits to a nonprofit entity and receive a tax deduction. Usually, the government lowers the overall limit over time, with an aim towards a national emissions reduction target.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cap101_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-cap101-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the Environmental Defense Fund, cap-and-trade is the most environmentally and economically sensible approach to controlling greenhouse gas emissions, the primary cause of global warming, because it sets a limit on emissions, and the trading encourages companies to innovate in order to emit less.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>"International trade can offer a range of positive and negative incentives to promote international cooperation on climate change (robust evidence, medium agreement). Three issues are key to developing constructive relationships between international trade and climate agreements: how existing trade policies and rules can be modified to be more climate friendly; whether border adjustment measures (BAMs) or other trade measures can be effective in meeting the goals of international climate agreements; whether the UNFCCC, World Trade Organization (WTO), hybrid of the two, or a new institution is the best forum for a trade-and-climate architecture."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-report.mitigation2014.org_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-report.mitigation2014.org-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Market_and_least-cost">Market and least-cost</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emissions_trading&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Market and least-cost">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:12em; ;"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303512">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{margin:0.5em auto 0.8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft p,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright p{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:larger;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" “ ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" ” ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:360px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{min-width:100%;margin:0 0 0.8em!important;float:none!important}}</style>
<div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">Economy-wide pricing of carbon is the centre piece of any policy designed to reduce emissions at the lowest possible costs.</div>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style=""><a href="/wiki/Ross_Garnaut" title="Ross Garnaut">Ross Garnaut</a>, lead author of the <a href="/wiki/Garnaut_Climate_Change_Review" title="Garnaut Climate Change Review">Garnaut Climate Change Review</a> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kpup6_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-kpup6-20">[20]</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>Some economists have urged the use of market-based instruments such as emissions trading to address environmental problems instead of prescriptive "command-and-control" regulation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stavins98_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-stavins98-21">[21]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Command_and_control_regulation" title="Command and control regulation">Command and control regulation</a> is criticized for being insensitive to geographical and technological differences, and therefore inefficient.;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> however, this is not always so, as shown by the WW-II rationing program in the U.S. in which local and regional boards made adjustments for these differences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>After an emissions limit has been set by a government political process, individual companies are free to choose how or whether to reduce their emissions. Failure to report emissions and surrender emission permits is often punishable by a further government regulatory mechanism, such as a fine that increases costs of production. Firms will choose the least-cost way to comply with the pollution regulation, which will lead to reductions where the least expensive solutions exist, while allowing emissions that are more expensive to reduce.
</p><p>Under an emissions trading system, each regulated polluter has flexibility to use the most cost-effective combination of buying or selling emission permits, reducing its emissions by installing cleaner technology, or reducing its emissions by reducing production. The most cost-effective strategy depends on the polluter's marginal abatement cost and the market price of permits. In theory, a polluter's decisions should lead to an economically efficient allocation of reductions among polluters, and lower compliance costs for individual firms and for the economy overall, compared to command-and-control mechanisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-boswall_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-boswall-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Emission_markets">Emission markets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emissions_trading&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Emission markets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>For emissions trading where greenhouse gases are regulated, one emissions permit is considered equivalent to one <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metric_ton" title="Metric ton">metric ton</a> of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> (CO<sub>2</sub>) emissions. Other names for emissions permits are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_credits" title="Carbon credits">carbon credits</a>, Kyoto units, <a href="/wiki/Assigned_amount_units" title="Assigned amount units">assigned amount units</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Certified_Emission_Reduction" title="Certified Emission Reduction">Certified Emission Reduction</a> units (CER). These permits can be sold privately or in the international market at the prevailing market price. These trade and <a href="/wiki/Settlement_(finance)" title="Settlement (finance)">settle</a> internationally, and hence allow permits to be transferred between countries. Each international transfer is validated by the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Framework_Convention_on_Climate_Change" title="United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change">United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change</a> (UNFCCC). Each transfer of ownership within the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> is additionally validated by the <a href="/wiki/European_Commission" title="European Commission">European Commission</a>.
</p><p>Emissions trading programmes such as the European Union Emissions Trading System (EU ETS) complement the country-to-country trading stipulated in the Kyoto Protocol by allowing private trading of permits. Under such programmes – which are generally co-ordinated with the national emissions targets provided within the framework of the Kyoto Protocol – a national or international authority allocates permits to individual companies based on established criteria, with a view to meeting national and/or regional Kyoto targets at the lowest overall economic cost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>Trading exchanges have been established to provide a <a href="/wiki/Spot_market" title="Spot market">spot market</a> in permits, as well as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Futures_market" title="Futures market">futures</a> and <a href="/wiki/Option_(finance)" title="Option (finance)">options</a> <a href="/wiki/Futures_exchange" title="Futures exchange">market</a> to help discover a market price and maintain <a href="/wiki/Market_liquidity" title="Market liquidity">liquidity</a>. Carbon prices are normally quoted in <a href="/wiki/Euro" title="Euro">euros</a> per tonne of carbon dioxide or its equivalent (CO<sub>2</sub>e). Other greenhouse gases can also be traded, but are quoted as standard multiples of carbon dioxide with respect to their <a href="/wiki/Global_warming_potential" title="Global warming potential">global warming potential</a>. These features reduce the quota's financial impact on business, while ensuring that the quotas are met at a national and international level.
</p><p>Currently, there are six exchanges trading in UNFCCC related <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_credits" title="Carbon credits">carbon credits</a>: the <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Climate_Exchange" title="Chicago Climate Exchange">Chicago Climate Exchange</a> (until 2010<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ng2010_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-ng2010-26">[26]</a></sup>), <a href="/wiki/European_Climate_Exchange" title="European Climate Exchange">European Climate Exchange</a>, <a href="/wiki/NASDAQ_OMX_Commodities_Europe" title="NASDAQ OMX Commodities Europe">NASDAQ OMX Commodities Europe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Powernext" title="Powernext">PowerNext</a>, <a href="/wiki/Commodity_Exchange_Bratislava" title="Commodity Exchange Bratislava">Commodity Exchange Bratislava</a> and the <a href="/wiki/European_Energy_Exchange" title="European Energy Exchange">European Energy Exchange</a>. NASDAQ OMX Commodities Europe listed a contract to trade offsets generated by a CDM <a href="/wiki/Carbon_project" title="Carbon project">carbon project</a> called Certified Emission Reductions. Many companies now engage in emissions abatement, offsetting, and sequestration programs to generate credits that can be sold on one of the exchanges. At least one <a href="/wiki/Private_electronic_market" title="Private electronic market">private electronic market</a> has been established in 2008: CantorCO2e.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Carbon credits at Commodity Exchange Bratislava are traded at special platform called Carbon place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>Trading in emission permits is one of the fastest-growing segments in financial services in the <a href="/wiki/City_of_London" title="City of London">City of London</a> with a market estimated to be worth about €30 billion in 2007. Louis Redshaw, head of environmental markets at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Barclays_Capital" title="Barclays Capital">Barclays Capital</a>, predicts that "carbon will be the world's biggest commodity market, and it could become the world's biggest market overall."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pollution_markets">Pollution markets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emissions_trading&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Pollution markets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>An emission license directly confers a right to emit pollutants up to a certain rate.
In contrast, a <b>pollution license</b> for a given location confers the right to emit pollutants at a rate which will cause no more than a specified increase at the pollution-level. For concreteness, consider the following model.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Montgomery1972_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Montgomery1972-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p>

<p>As an example, consider three countries along a river (as in the <a href="/wiki/Fair_river_sharing" title="Fair river sharing">fair river sharing</a> setting).
</p>
<ul><li>Pollution in the upstream country is determined only by the emission of the upstream country: <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle q_{1}=e_{1}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle q_{1}=e_{1}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/144ed39ef9b932ccd7ad722c3b7163865f9ebe34" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:7.327ex; height:2.009ex;"/></span>.</li>
<li>Pollution in the middle country is determined by its own emission and by the emission of country 1: <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle q_{2}=e_{1}+e_{2}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<msub>
<mi>q</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<p>So the matrix <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle H}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</p><p>Each pollution-license for location <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle i}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

Therefore, a polluter that affects water quality at a number of points has to hold a portfolio of licenses covering all relevant monitoring-points. In the above example, if country 2 wants to emit a unit of pollutant, it should purchase two permits: one for location 2 and one for location 3.
</p><p>Montgomery shows that, while both markets lead to efficient license allocation, the market in pollution-licenses is more widely applicable than the market in emission-licenses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Montgomery1972_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-Montgomery1972-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emissions_trading&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The international community began the long process towards building effective international and domestic measures to tackle GHG emissions (carbon dioxide, methane, nitrous oxide, hydroflurocarbons, perfluorocarbons, sulphur hexafluoride) in response to the increasing assertions that global warming is happening due to man-made emissions and the uncertainty over its likely consequences. That process began in Rio in 1992, when 160 countries agreed the UN Framework Convention on Climate Change (UNFCCC). The UNFCCC is, as its title suggests, simply a framework; the necessary detail was left to be settled by the Conference of Parties (CoP) to the UNFCCC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-boswall_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-boswall-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>The efficiency of what later was to be called the "cap-and-trade" approach to <a href="/wiki/Air_pollution" title="Air pollution">air pollution</a> abatement was first demonstrated in a series of micro-economic computer simulation studies between 1967 and 1970 for the National Air Pollution Control Administration (predecessor to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency" title="United States Environmental Protection Agency">United States Environmental Protection Agency</a>'s Office of Air and Radiation) by Ellison Burton and William Sanjour. These studies used mathematical models of several cities and their emission sources in order to compare the cost and effectiveness of various control strategies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Each abatement strategy was compared with the "least-cost solution" produced by a computer optimization program to identify the least-costly combination of source reductions in order to achieve a given abatement goal. In each case it was found that the least-cost solution was dramatically less costly than the same amount of pollution reduction produced by any conventional abatement strategy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> Burton and later Sanjour along with Edward H. Pechan continued improving <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> and advancing<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> these computer models at the newly created U.S. Environmental Protection Agency.  The agency introduced the concept of computer modeling with least-cost abatement strategies (i.e., emissions trading) in its 1972 annual report to Congress on the cost of clean air.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> This led to the concept of "cap and trade" as a means of achieving the "least-cost solution" for a given level of abatement.
</p><p>The development of emissions trading over the course of its history can be divided into four phases:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li>Gestation: Theoretical articulation of the instrument (by <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Coase" title="Ronald Coase">Coase</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Crocker,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> Dales,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Montgomery<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Montgomery1972_10-3"><a href="#cite_note-Montgomery1972-10">[10]</a></sup> etc.) and, independent of the former, tinkering with "flexible regulation" at the US Environmental Protection Agency.</li>
<li>Proof of Principle: First developments towards trading of emission certificates based on the "offset-mechanism" taken up in Clean Air Act in 1977. A company could get allowance from the Act on a greater amount of emission when it paid another company to reduce the same pollutant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></li>
<li>Prototype: Launching of a first "cap-and-trade" system as part of the US <a href="/wiki/Acid_Rain_Program" title="Acid Rain Program">Acid Rain Program</a> in Title IV of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1990_Clean_Air_Act" title="1990 Clean Air Act">1990 Clean Air Act</a>, officially announced as a paradigm shift in environmental policy, as prepared by "Project 88", a network-building effort to bring together environmental and industrial interests in the US.</li>
<li>Regime formation: branching out from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Center_for_Clean_Air_Policy" title="The Center for Clean Air Policy">US clean air policy</a> to <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_policy_of_the_United_States" title="Climate change policy of the United States">global climate policy</a>, and from there to the European Union, along with the expectation of an emerging global <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> market and the formation of the "carbon industry".</li></ol>
<p>In the United States, the "<a href="/wiki/Acid_rain" title="Acid rain">acid rain</a>"-related emission trading system was principally conceived by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boyden_Gray" title="Boyden Gray">C. Boyden Gray</a>, a <a href="/wiki/George_H._W._Bush" title="George H. W. Bush">G.H.W. Bush</a> administration attorney. Gray worked with the <a href="/wiki/Environmental_Defense_Fund" title="Environmental Defense Fund">Environmental Defense Fund</a> (EDF), who worked with the EPA to write the bill that became law as part of the Clean Air Act of 1990. The new emissions cap on NO<sub>x</sub> and <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_dioxide" title="Sulfur dioxide"><span class="chemf nowrap">SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span></a> gases took effect in 1995, and according to <i><a href="/wiki/Smithsonian_(magazine)" title="Smithsonian (magazine)">Smithsonian</a></i> magazine, those acid rain emissions dropped 3 million tons that year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> In 1997, the CoP agreed, in what has been described as a watershed in international environmental treaty making, the Kyoto Protocol where 38 developed countries (Annex 1 countries) committed themselves to targets and timetables for the reduction of GHGs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> These targets for developed countries are often referred to as Assigned Amounts.
</p><p>One important economic reality recognised by many of the countries that signed the Kyoto Protocol is that, if countries have to solely rely on their own domestic measures, the resulting inflexible limitations on GHG growth could entail very large costs, perhaps running into many trillions of dollars globally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> As a result, international mechanisms which would allow developed countries flexibility to meet their targets were included in the Kyoto Protocol. The purpose of these mechanisms is to allow the parties to find the most economical ways to achieve their targets. These international mechanisms are outlined under Kyoto Protocol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-boswall_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-boswall-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>On April 17, 2009, the Environmental Protection Agency (EPA) formally announced that it had found that greenhouse gas (GHG) poses a threat to public health and the environment (EPA 2009a). This announcement was significant because it gives the executive branch the authority to impose carbon regulations on carbon-emitting entities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roos_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roos-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>A carbon cap-and-trade system is to be introduced nationwide in China in 2016<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> (China's National Development and Reform Commission proposed that an absolute cap be placed on emission by 2016.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_opinion">Public opinion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emissions_trading&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Public opinion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In the United States, most polling shows large support for emissions trading (often referred to as cap-and-trade). This majority support can be seen in polls conducted by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Washington_Post" title="Washington Post">Washington Post</a>/<a href="/wiki/ABC_News" title="ABC News">ABC News</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zogby_International" title="Zogby International">Zogby International</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Yale_University" title="Yale University">Yale University</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> A new Washington Post-ABC poll reveals that majorities of the American people believe in climate change, are concerned about it, are willing to change their lifestyles and pay more to address it, and want the federal government to regulate greenhouse gases. They are, however, ambivalent on cap-and-trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grist.org_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-grist.org-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>More than three-quarters of respondents, 77.0%, reported they “strongly support” (51.0%) or “somewhat support” (26.0%) the EPA’s decision to regulate carbon emissions. While 68.6% of respondents reported being “very willing” (23.0%) or “somewhat willing” (45.6%), another 26.8% reported being “somewhat unwilling” (8.8%) or “not at all willing” (18.0%) to pay higher prices for “Green” energy sources to support funding for programs that reduce the effect of global warming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grist.org_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-grist.org-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/PolitiFact" title="PolitiFact">PolitiFact</a>, it is a misconception that emissions trading is unpopular in the United States because of earlier polls from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IBOPE_Zogby_International" title="IBOPE Zogby International">Zogby International</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rasmussen" title="Rasmussen">Rasmussen</a> which misleadingly include "new taxes" in the questions (taxes aren't part of emissions trading) or high energy cost estimates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Comparison_with_other_methods_of_emission_reduction">Comparison with other methods of emission reduction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emissions_trading&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Comparison with other methods of emission reduction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Cap and trade is the textbook emissions trading program. Other market-based approaches include baseline-and-credit, and pollution tax. They all put a price on pollution (for example, see <a href="/wiki/Carbon_price" title="Carbon price">carbon price</a>), and so provide an economic incentive to reduce pollution beginning with the lowest-cost opportunities. By contrast, in a command-and-control approach, a central authority designates pollution levels each facility is allowed to emit.  Cap and trade essentially functions as a tax where the tax rate is variable based on the relative cost of abatement per unit, and the tax base is variable based on the amount of abatement needed.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Baseline_and_credit">Baseline and credit</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emissions_trading&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Baseline and credit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In a baseline and credit program, polluters can create permits, called credits or offsets, by reducing their emissions below a baseline level, which is often the historical emissions level from a designated past year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-glossary_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-glossary-4">[4]</a></sup> Such credits can be bought by polluters that have a regulatory limit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pollution_tax">Pollution tax</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emissions_trading&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Pollution tax">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Emissions fees or environmental tax is a surcharge on the pollution created while producing goods and services.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rosengayer_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-rosengayer-56">[56]</a></sup> For example, a carbon tax is a tax on the carbon content of fossil fuels that aims to discourage their use and thereby reduce carbon dioxide emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-glossary_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-glossary-4">[4]</a></sup> The two approaches are overlapping sets of policy designs. Both can have a range of scopes, points of regulation, and price schedules. They can be fair or unfair, depending on how the revenue is used. Both have the effect of increasing the price of goods (such as fossil fuels) to consumers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-57">[57]</a></sup>  A comprehensive, upstream, auctioned cap-and-trade system is very similar to a comprehensive, upstream carbon tax. Yet, many commentators sharply contrast the two approaches.
</p><p>The main difference is what is defined and what derived. A tax is a price control, while cap-and-trade method acts is a quantity control instrument.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-57">[57]</a></sup> That is, a tax is a unit price for pollution that is set by authorities, and the market determines the quantity emitted; in cap and trade, authorities determine the amount of pollution, and the market determines the price.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> This difference affects a number of criteria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rosengayer_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-rosengayer-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p><b>Responsiveness to inflation:</b> Cap-and-trade has the advantage that it adjusts to <a href="/wiki/Inflation" title="Inflation">inflation</a> (changes to overall prices) automatically, while emissions fees must be changed by regulators.
</p><p><b>Responsiveness to cost changes:</b> It is not clear which approach is better. It is possible to combine the two into a safety valve price: a price set by regulators, at which polluters can buy additional permits beyond the cap.
</p><p><b>Responsiveness to recessions:</b> This point is closely related to responsiveness to cost changes, because recessions cause a drop in demand. Under cap and trade, the emissions cost automatically decreases, so a cap-and-trade scheme adds another <a href="/wiki/Automatic_stabilizer" title="Automatic stabilizer">automatic stabilizer</a> to the economy - in effect, an automatic fiscal stimulus. However, a lower pollution price also results in reduced efforts to reduce pollution. If the government is able to stimulate the economy regardless of the cap-and-trade scheme, an excessively low price causes a missed opportunity to cut emissions faster than planned. Instead, it might be better to have a price floor (a tax). This is especially true when cutting pollution is urgent, as with greenhouse gas emissions. A price floor also provides certainty and stability for investment in emissions reductions: recent experience from the UK shows that nuclear power operators are reluctant to invest on "un-subsidised" terms unless there is a guaranteed price floor for carbon (which the EU emissions trading scheme does not presently provide).
</p><p><b>Responsiveness to uncertainty:</b> As with cost changes, in a world of uncertainty, it is not clear whether emissions fees or cap-and-trade systems are more efficient—it depends on how fast the marginal social benefits of reducing pollution fall with the amount of cleanup (e.g., whether inelastic or elastic marginal social benefit schedule).
</p><p><b>Other:</b> The magnitude of the tax will depend on how sensitive the supply of emissions is to the price. The permit price of cap-and-trade will depend on the pollutant market. A tax generates government revenue, but full-auctioned emissions permits can do the same. A similar upstream cap-and-trade system could be implemented. An upstream carbon tax might be the simplest to administer. Setting up a complex cap-and-trade arrangement that is comprehensive has high institutional needs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Command-and-control_regulation">Command-and-control regulation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emissions_trading&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Command-and-control regulation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Command and control is a system of regulation that prescribes emission limits and compliance methods for each facility or source. It is the traditional approach to reducing air pollution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-glossary_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-glossary-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>Command-and-control regulations are more rigid than incentive-based approaches such as pollution fees and cap and trade. An example of this is a performance standard which sets an emissions goal for each polluter that is fixed and, therefore, the burden of reducing pollution cannot be shifted to the firms that can achieve it more cheaply. As a result, performance standards are likely to be more costly overall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rosengayer_56-2"><a href="#cite_note-rosengayer-56">[56]</a></sup> The additional costs would be passed to end consumers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economics_of_international_emissions_trading">Economics of international emissions trading</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emissions_trading&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Economics of international emissions trading">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

</tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">
<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left;border-bottom: 1px solid #ccc; background: transparent; text-align: left;"><span style="color:#0645AD">Theory</span></div><div class="NavContent hlist" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Comparative_advantage" title="Comparative advantage">Comparative advantage</a></li>

<li><a href="/wiki/Terms_of_trade" title="Terms of trade">Terms of trade</a></li></ul></div></div></td>
</tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:World_trade" title="Template:World trade"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:World_trade" title="Template talk:World trade"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:World_trade&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>It is possible for a country to reduce emissions using a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Command_and_Control_(government)" title="Command and Control (government)">Command-Control</a> approach, such as regulation, <a href="/wiki/Direct_tax" title="Direct tax">direct</a> and <a href="/wiki/Indirect_tax" title="Indirect tax">indirect taxes</a>. The cost of that approach differs between countries because the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marginal_Abatement_Cost_Curve" title="Marginal Abatement Cost Curve">Marginal Abatement Cost Curve</a> (MAC) — the cost of eliminating an additional unit of pollution — differs by country. It might cost China $2 to eliminate a ton of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CO2" title="CO2">CO<sub>2</sub></a>, but it would probably cost Norway or the U.S. much more. International emissions-trading markets were created precisely to exploit differing MACs.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Example">Example</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emissions_trading&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Example">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Emissions trading through <a href="/wiki/Gains_from_trade" title="Gains from trade"><i>Gains from Trade</i></a> can be more beneficial for both the buyer and the seller than a simple emissions capping scheme.
</p><p>Consider two European countries, such as Germany and Sweden. Each can either reduce all the required amount of emissions by itself or it can choose to buy or sell in the market.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Emissions.Trading.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="917" data-file-width="1390" height="264" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Emissions.Trading.jpeg/400px-Emissions.Trading.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Emissions.Trading.jpeg/600px-Emissions.Trading.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Emissions.Trading.jpeg/800px-Emissions.Trading.jpeg 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Emissions.Trading.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Example MACs for two different countries</div></div></div>
<p>Suppose Germany can abate its CO<sub>2</sub> at a much cheaper cost than Sweden, i.e. MAC<sub>S</sub> &gt; MAC<sub>G</sub> where the MAC curve of Sweden is steeper (higher slope) than that of Germany, and R<sub>Req</sub> is the total amount of emissions that need to be reduced by a country.
</p><p>On the left side of the graph is the MAC curve for Germany. R<sub>Req</sub> is the amount of required reductions for Germany, but at R<sub>Req</sub> the MAC<sub>G</sub> curve has not intersected the market emissions permit price of CO<sub>2</sub> (market permit price = P = λ). Thus, given the market price of CO<sub>2</sub> allowances, Germany has potential to profit if it abates more emissions than required.
</p><p>On the right side is the MAC curve for Sweden. R<sub>Req</sub> is the amount of required reductions for Sweden, but the MAC<sub>S</sub> curve already intersects the market price of CO<sub>2</sub> permits before R<sub>Req</sub> has been reached. Thus, given the market price of CO<sub>2</sub> permits, Sweden has potential to make a cost saving if it abates fewer emissions than required internally, and instead abates them elsewhere.
</p><p>In this example, Sweden would abate emissions until its MAC<sub>S</sub> intersects with P (at R*), but this would only reduce a fraction of Sweden's total required abatement.
</p><p>After that it could buy emissions credits from Germany for the price <i>P</i> (per unit). The internal cost of Sweden's own abatement, combined with the permits it buys in the market from Germany, adds up to the total required reductions (R<sub>Req</sub>) for Sweden. Thus Sweden can make a saving from buying permits in the market (Δ d-e-f). This represents the "Gains from Trade", the amount of additional expense that Sweden would otherwise have to spend if it abated all of its required emissions by itself without trading.
</p><p>Germany made a profit on its additional emissions abatement, above what was required: it met the regulations by abating all of the emissions that was required of it (R<sub>Req</sub>). Additionally, Germany sold its surplus permits to Sweden, and was paid <i>P</i> for every unit it abated, while spending less than <i>P</i>. Its total revenue is the area of the graph (R<sub>Req</sub> 1 2 R*), its total abatement cost is area (R<sub>Req</sub> 3 2 R*), and so its net benefit from selling emission permits is the area (Δ 1-2-3) i.e. Gains from Trade
</p><p>The two R* (on both graphs) represent the efficient allocations that arise from trading.
</p>
<ul><li>Germany: sold (R* - R<sub>Req</sub>) emission permits to Sweden at a unit price <i>P</i>.</li>
<li>Sweden bought emission permits from Germany at a unit price <i>P</i>.</li></ul>
<p>If the total cost for reducing a particular amount of emissions in the <i>Command Control</i> scenario is called <i>X</i>, then to reduce the same amount of combined pollution in Sweden and Germany, the total abatement cost would be less in the <i>Emissions Trading</i> scenario i.e. (X — Δ 123 - Δ def).
</p><p>The example above applies not just at the national level, but also between two companies in different countries, or between two subsidiaries within the same company.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Applying_the_economic_theory">Applying the economic theory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emissions_trading&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Applying the economic theory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The nature of the pollutant plays a very important role when policy-makers decide which framework should be used to control pollution. CO<sub>2</sub> acts globally, thus its impact on the environment is generally similar wherever in the globe it is released. So the location of the originator of the emissions does not matter from an environmental standpoint.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>The policy framework should be different for regional pollutants<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_dioxide" title="Sulfur dioxide">SO<sub>2</sub></a> and <a href="/wiki/NOx" title="NOx">NO<sub>x</sub></a>, and also <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a>) because the impact of these pollutants may differ by location. The same amount of a regional pollutant can exert a very high impact in some locations and a low impact in other locations, so it matters where the pollutant is released. This is known as the <i>Hot Spot</i> problem.
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Euler%E2%80%93Lagrange_equation" title="Euler–Lagrange equation">Lagrange framework</a> is commonly used to determine the least cost of achieving an objective, in this case the total reduction in emissions required in a year. In some cases, it is possible to use the Lagrange optimization framework to determine the required reductions for each country (based on their MAC) so that the total cost of reduction is minimized. In such a scenario, the <a href="/wiki/Lagrange_multiplier" title="Lagrange multiplier">Lagrange multiplier</a> represents the market allowance price (P) of a pollutant, such as the current market  price of emission permits in Europe and the USA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>Countries face the permit market price that exists in the market that day, so they are able to make individual decisions that would minimize their costs while at the same time achieving regulatory compliance. This is also another version of the <a href="/wiki/Ordinal_utility" title="Ordinal utility">Equi-Marginal Principle</a>, commonly used in economics to choose the most economically efficient decision.
</p>
<h3><span id="Prices_versus_quantities.2C_and_the_safety_valve"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Prices_versus_quantities,_and_the_safety_valve">Prices versus quantities, and the safety valve</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emissions_trading&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Prices versus quantities, and the safety valve">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There has been longstanding debate on the relative merits of <i>price</i> versus <i>quantity</i> instruments to achieve emission reductions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>An emission cap and permit trading system is a <i>quantity</i> instrument because it fixes the overall emission level (quantity) and allows the price to vary. Uncertainty in future supply and demand conditions (market volatility) coupled with a fixed number of pollution permits creates an uncertainty in the future price of pollution permits, and the industry must accordingly bear the cost of adapting to these volatile market conditions. The burden of a volatile market thus lies with the industry rather than the controlling agency, which is generally more efficient. However, under volatile market conditions, the ability of the controlling agency to alter the caps will translate into an ability to pick "winners and losers" and thus presents an opportunity for corruption.
</p><p>In contrast, an <a href="/wiki/Carbon_tax" title="Carbon tax">emission tax</a> is a <i>price</i> instrument because it fixes the price while the emission level is allowed to vary according to economic activity. A major drawback of an emission tax is that the environmental outcome (e.g. a limit on the amount of emissions) is not guaranteed. On one hand, a tax will remove capital from the industry, suppressing possibly useful economic activity, but conversely, the polluter will not need to hedge as much against future uncertainty since the amount of tax will track with profits. The burden of a volatile market will be borne by the controlling (taxing) agency rather than the industry itself, which is generally less efficient. An advantage is that, given a uniform tax rate and a volatile market, the taxing entity will not be in a position to pick "winners and losers" and the opportunity for corruption will be less.
</p><p>Assuming no corruption and assuming that the controlling agency and the industry are equally efficient at adapting to volatile market conditions, the best choice depends on the sensitivity of the costs of emission reduction, compared to the sensitivity of the benefits (i.e., climate damage avoided by a reduction) when the level of emission control is varied.
</p><p>Because there is high uncertainty in the compliance costs of firms, some argue that the optimum choice is the price mechanism. However, the burden of uncertainty cannot be eliminated, and in this case it is shifted to the taxing agency itself.
</p><p>The overwhelming majority of climate scientists have repeatedly warned of a threshold in atmospheric concentrations of carbon dioxide beyond which a run-away <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">warming</a> effect could take place, with a large possibility of causing irreversible damage. With such a risk, a quantity instrument may be a better choice because the quantity of emissions may be capped with more certainty. However, this may not be true if this risk exists but cannot be attached to a known level of greenhouse gas (GHG) concentration or a known emission pathway.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>A third option, known as a <i>safety valve</i>, is a hybrid of the price and quantity instruments. The system is essentially an emission cap and permit trading system but the maximum (or minimum) permit price is capped. Emitters have the choice of either obtaining permits in the marketplace or buying them from the government at a specified trigger price (which could be adjusted over time). The system is sometimes recommended as a way of overcoming the fundamental disadvantages of both systems by giving governments the flexibility to adjust the system as new information comes to light. It can be shown that by setting the trigger price high enough, or the number of permits low enough, the safety valve can be used to mimic either a pure quantity or pure price mechanism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>All three methods are being used as policy instruments to control greenhouse gas emissions: the EU-ETS is a <i>quantity</i> system using the cap and trading system to meet targets set by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Allocation_Plan" title="National Allocation Plan">National Allocation Plans</a>; Denmark has a price system using a <a href="/wiki/Carbon_tax" title="Carbon tax">carbon tax</a> (World Bank, 2010, p. 218),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wdr_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-wdr-67">[67]</a></sup> while China uses the CO<sub>2</sub> market price for funding of its <a href="/wiki/Clean_Development_Mechanism" title="Clean Development Mechanism">Clean Development Mechanism</a> projects, but imposes a <i>safety valve</i> of a minimum price per tonne of CO<sub>2</sub>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Carbon_leakage">Carbon leakage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emissions_trading&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Carbon leakage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Carbon_leakage" title="Carbon leakage">Carbon leakage</a> is the effect that regulation of emissions in one country/sector has on the emissions in other countries/sectors that are not subject to the same regulation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-barker_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-barker-68">[68]</a></sup> There is no consensus over the magnitude of long-term carbon leakage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the Kyoto Protocol, Annex I countries are subject to caps on emissions, but non-Annex I countries are not. Barker <i>et al.</i> (2007) assessed the literature on leakage. The leakage rate is defined as the increase in CO<sub>2</sub> emissions outside the countries taking domestic mitigation action, divided by the reduction in emissions of countries taking domestic mitigation action. Accordingly, a leakage rate greater than 100% means that actions to reduce emissions within countries had the effect of increasing emissions in other countries to a greater extent, i.e., domestic mitigation action had actually led to an increase in global emissions.
</p><p>Estimates of leakage rates for action under the Kyoto Protocol ranged from 5% to 20% as a result of a loss in price competitiveness, but these leakage rates were considered very uncertain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-barker_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-barker-68">[68]</a></sup> For energy-intensive industries, the beneficial effects of Annex I actions through technological development were considered possibly substantial. However, this beneficial effect had not been reliably quantified. On the empirical evidence they assessed, Barker <i>et al.</i> (2007) concluded that the competitive losses of then-current mitigation actions, e.g., the EU ETS, were not significant.
</p><p>Under the EU ETS rules <a class="external text" href="http://www.emissions-euets.com/carbon-leakage-exposure-factor-clef" rel="nofollow">Carbon Leakage Exposure Factor</a> is used to determine the volumes of free allocation of emission permits to industrial installations.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trade">Trade</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emissions_trading&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Trade">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>To understand carbon trading, it is important to understand the products that are being traded. The primary product in carbon markets is the trading of GHG emission permits. Under a cap-and-trade system, permits are issued to various entities for the right to emit GHG emissions that meet emission reduction requirement caps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roos_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-Roos-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>One of the controversies about carbon mitigation policy is how to "level the playing field" with border adjustments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> For example, one component of the <a href="/wiki/American_Clean_Energy_and_Security_Act" title="American Clean Energy and Security Act">American Clean Energy and Security Act</a> (a 2009 bill that did not pass), along with several other energy bills put before US Congress, calls for carbon surcharges on goods imported from countries without cap-and-trade programs. Besides issues of compliance with the <a href="/wiki/General_Agreement_on_Tariffs_and_Trade" title="General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade">General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade</a>, such border adjustments presume that the producing countries bear responsibility for the carbon emissions.
</p><p>A general perception among developing countries is that discussion of climate change in trade negotiations could lead to "green <a href="/wiki/Protectionism" title="Protectionism">protectionism</a>" by high-income countries (World Bank, 2010, p. 251).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wdr_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-wdr-67">[67]</a></sup> Tariffs on imports ("virtual carbon") consistent with a carbon price of $50 per ton of CO<sub>2</sub> could be significant for developing countries. World Bank (2010) commented that introducing border tariffs could lead to a proliferation of trade measures where the competitive playing field is viewed as being uneven. Tariffs could also be a burden on low-income countries that have contributed very little to the problem of climate change.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Trading_systems">Trading systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emissions_trading&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Trading systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ETS_and_carbon_tax_world_map.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1314" data-file-width="2560" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/ETS_and_carbon_tax_world_map.svg/350px-ETS_and_carbon_tax_world_map.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/ETS_and_carbon_tax_world_map.svg/525px-ETS_and_carbon_tax_world_map.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/ETS_and_carbon_tax_world_map.svg/700px-ETS_and_carbon_tax_world_map.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ETS_and_carbon_tax_world_map.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Carbon emission trading and carbon tax around the world<br/> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #2ca25f; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> Emission trading implemented</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #addd8e; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> Emission trading scheduled</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #d95f0e; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> Carbon tax implemented</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #fec44f; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> Carbon tax scheduled</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #e8afe2; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> Emission trading or carbon tax under consideration</div></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kyoto_Protocol">Kyoto Protocol</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emissions_trading&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Kyoto Protocol">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1990, the first <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_Panel_on_Climate_Change" title="Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change">Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change</a> (IPCC) report highlighted the imminent threat of climate change and greenhouse gas emission, and diplomatic efforts began to find an international framework within which such emissions could be regulated. In 1997 the Kyoto Protocol was adopted.
The <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_Protocol" title="Kyoto Protocol">Kyoto Protocol</a> is a 1997 international treaty that came into force in 2005. In the treaty, most developed nations agreed to legally binding targets for their emissions of the six major <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gases" title="Greenhouse gases">greenhouse gases</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Emission quotas (known as "Assigned amounts") were agreed by each participating 'Annex I' country, with the intention of reducing the overall emissions by 5.2% from their 1990 levels by the end of 2012. Between 1990 and 2012 the original Kyoto Protocol parties reduced their CO2 emissions by 12.5%, which is well beyond the 2012 target of 4.7%. The United States is the only industrialized nation under <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Framework_Convention_on_Climate_Change#Annex_I_countries" title="United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change">Annex I</a> that has not ratified the treaty, and is therefore not bound by it. The IPCC has projected that the financial effect of compliance through trading within the Kyoto commitment period will be limited at between 0.1-1.1% of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GDP" title="GDP">GDP</a> among trading countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>
The agreement was intended to result in industrialized countries' emissions declining in aggregate by 5.2 percent below 1990 levels by the year of 2012. Despite the failure of the United States and Australia to ratify the protocol, the agreement became effective in 2005, once the requirement that 55 Annex I (predominantly industrialized) countries, jointly accounting for 55 percent of 1990 Annex I emissions, ratify the agreement was met.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Protocol defines several mechanisms ("<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flexible_mechanisms" title="Flexible mechanisms">flexible mechanisms</a>") that are designed to allow Annex I countries to meet their emission reduction commitments (caps) with reduced economic impact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>Under Article 3.3 of the Kyoto Protocol, Annex I Parties may use GHG removals, from afforestation and reforestation (forest sinks) and deforestation (sources) since 1990, to meet their emission reduction commitments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>Annex I Parties may also use International Emissions Trading (IET). Under the treaty, for the 5-year compliance period from 2008 until 2012,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> nations that emit less than their quota will be able to sell <a href="/wiki/Assigned_amount_units" title="Assigned amount units">assigned amount units</a> (each AAU
representing an allowance to emit one metric tonne of CO<sub>2</sub>) to nations that exceed their quotas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> It is also possible for Annex I countries to sponsor <a href="/wiki/Carbon_project" title="Carbon project">carbon projects</a> that reduce greenhouse gas emissions in other countries. These projects generate tradable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_credits" title="Carbon credits">carbon credits</a> that can be used by Annex I countries in meeting their caps. The project-based Kyoto Mechanisms are the <a href="/wiki/Clean_Development_Mechanism" title="Clean Development Mechanism">Clean Development Mechanism</a> (CDM) and <a href="/wiki/Joint_Implementation" title="Joint Implementation">Joint Implementation</a> (JI). There are four such international flexible mechanisms, or Kyoto Mechanism,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> written in the Kyoto Protocol.
</p><p>Article 17 if the Protocol authorizes Annex 1 countries that have agreed to the emissions limitations to take part in emissions trading with other Annex 1 Countries.
</p><p>Article 4 authorizes such parties to implement their limitations jointly, as the member states of the EU have chosen to do.
</p><p>Article 6 provides that such Annex 1 countries may take part in joint initiatives (JIs) in return for emissions reduction units (ERUs) to be used against their Assigned Amounts.
</p><p>Art 12 provides for a mechanism known as the clean development mechanism (CDM),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> under which Annex 1 countries may invest in emissions limitation projects in developing countries and use certified emissions reductions (CERs) generated against their own Assigned Amounts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-boswall_15-4"><a href="#cite_note-boswall-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>The CDM covers projects taking place in non-Annex I countries, while JI covers projects taking place in Annex I countries. CDM projects are supposed to contribute to <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_development" title="Sustainable development">sustainable development</a> in developing countries, and also generate "real" and "additional" emission savings, i.e., savings that only occur thanks to the CDM project in question (Carbon Trust, 2009, p. 14).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> Whether or not these emission savings are genuine is, however, difficult to prove (World Bank, 2010, pp. 265–267).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wdr_67-2"><a href="#cite_note-wdr-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Australia">Australia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emissions_trading&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Australia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Garnaut_Climate_Change_Review" title="Garnaut Climate Change Review">Garnaut Climate Change Review</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_Pollution_Reduction_Scheme" title="Carbon Pollution Reduction Scheme">Carbon Pollution Reduction Scheme</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clean_Energy_Bill_2011" title="Clean Energy Bill 2011">Clean Energy Bill 2011</a></div>
<p>In 2003 the New South Wales (NSW) state government unilaterally established the NSW Greenhouse Gas Abatement Scheme<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> to reduce emissions by requiring electricity generators and large consumers to purchase NSW Greenhouse Abatement Certificates (NGACs). This has prompted the rollout of free energy-efficient compact fluorescent lightbulbs and other energy-efficiency measures, funded by the credits. This scheme has been criticised by the Centre for Energy and Environmental Markets (CEEM) of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UNSW" title="UNSW">UNSW</a> because of its lack of effectiveness in reducing emissions, its lack of transparency and its lack of verification of the additionality of emission reductions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>Both the incumbent <a href="/wiki/John_Howard" title="John Howard">Howard</a> <a href="/wiki/Coalition_(Australia)" title="Coalition (Australia)">Coalition</a> government and the <a href="/wiki/Kevin_Rudd" title="Kevin Rudd">Rudd</a> <a href="/wiki/Australian_Labor_Party" title="Australian Labor Party">Labor</a> opposition promised to implement an emissions trading scheme (ETS) before the <a href="/wiki/Australian_federal_election,_2007" title="Australian federal election, 2007">2007 federal election</a>. Labor won the election, with the new government proceeding to implement an ETS. The government introduced the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_Pollution_Reduction_Scheme" title="Carbon Pollution Reduction Scheme">Carbon Pollution Reduction Scheme</a>, which the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_of_Australia" title="Liberal Party of Australia">Liberals</a> supported with <a href="/wiki/Malcolm_Turnbull" title="Malcolm Turnbull">Malcolm Turnbull</a> as leader. <a href="/wiki/Tony_Abbott" title="Tony Abbott">Tony Abbott</a> questioned an ETS, saying the best way to reduce emissions is with a "simple tax".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> Shortly before the carbon vote, Abbott defeated Turnbull in a leadership challenge, and from there on the Liberals opposed the ETS. This left the government unable to secure passage of the bill and it was subsequently withdrawn.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Julia_Gillard" title="Julia Gillard">Julia Gillard</a> defeated Rudd in a leadership challenge and promised not to introduce a carbon tax, but would look to legislate a price on carbon <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> when taking the government to the <a href="/wiki/Australian_federal_election,_2010" title="Australian federal election, 2010">2010 election</a>. In the first <a href="/wiki/Hung_parliament" title="Hung parliament">hung parliament</a> result in 70 years, the government required the support of crossbenchers including the <a href="/wiki/Australian_Greens" title="Australian Greens">Greens</a>. One requirement for Greens support was a carbon price, which Gillard proceeded with in forming a minority government. A fixed carbon price would proceed to a floating-price ETS within a few years under the plan. The fixed price lent itself to characterisation as a carbon tax and when the government proposed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clean_Energy_Bill_2011" title="Clean Energy Bill 2011">Clean Energy Bill</a> in February 2011,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> the opposition claimed it to be a broken election promise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p><p>The bill was passed by the <a href="/wiki/Australian_House_of_Representatives" title="Australian House of Representatives">Lower House</a> in October 2011<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Australian_Senate" title="Australian Senate">Upper House</a> in November 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> The Liberal Party vowed to overturn the bill if elected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> The bill thus resulted in passage of the Clean Energy Act, which possessed a great deal of flexibility in its design and uncertainty over its future.
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liberal/National_coalition" title="Liberal/National coalition">Liberal/National coalition</a> government elected in September 2013 has promised to reverse the climate legislation of the previous government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> In July 2014, the carbon tax was repealed as well as the Emissions Trading Scheme (ETS) that was to start in 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_Zealand">New Zealand</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emissions_trading&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: New Zealand">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_Emissions_Trading_Scheme" title="New Zealand Emissions Trading Scheme">New Zealand Emissions Trading Scheme</a> and <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_in_New_Zealand" title="Climate change in New Zealand">Climate change in New Zealand</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NZU-NZ-emission-unit-720by540.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="540" data-file-width="720" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/NZU-NZ-emission-unit-720by540.svg/400px-NZU-NZ-emission-unit-720by540.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/NZU-NZ-emission-unit-720by540.svg/600px-NZU-NZ-emission-unit-720by540.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/NZU-NZ-emission-unit-720by540.svg/800px-NZU-NZ-emission-unit-720by540.svg.png 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NZU-NZ-emission-unit-720by540.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>New Zealand Unit Prices</div></div></div>
