<p>The New Zealand Emissions Trading Scheme (NZ ETS) is a partial-coverage all-free allocation uncapped highly internationally linked emissions trading scheme. The NZ ETS was first legislated in the <a href="/wiki/Climate_Change_Response_(Emissions_Trading)_Amendment_Act_2008" title="Climate Change Response (Emissions Trading) Amendment Act 2008">Climate Change Response (Emissions Trading) Amendment Act 2008</a> in September 2008 under the <a href="/wiki/Fifth_Labour_Government_of_New_Zealand" title="Fifth Labour Government of New Zealand">Fifth Labour Government of New Zealand</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-parker08_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-parker08-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ccret_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-ccret-93">[93]</a></sup> and then amended in November 2009<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smith251109_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-smith251109-94">[94]</a></sup> and in November 2012<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-groser12_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-groser12-95">[95]</a></sup> by the <a href="/wiki/Fifth_National_Government_of_New_Zealand" title="Fifth National Government of New Zealand">Fifth National Government of New Zealand</a>.
</p><p>The NZ ETS covers forestry (a net sink), energy (43.4% of total 2010 emissions), industry (6.7% of total 2010 emissions) and waste (2.8% of total 2010 emissions) but not pastoral agriculture (47% of 2010 total emissions).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-snapshot_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-snapshot-96">[96]</a></sup> Participants in the NZ ETS must surrender two emissions units (either an international 'Kyoto' unit or a New Zealand-issued unit) for every three tonnes of carbon dioxide equivalent emissions reported or they may choose to buy NZ units from the government at a fixed price of NZ$25.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MfESept09_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-MfESept09-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p>Individual sectors of the economy have different entry dates when their obligations to report emissions and surrender emission units take effect. Forestry, which contributed net removals of 17.5 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megatonnes#Derived_units" title="Megatonnes">Mts</a> of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_equivalent" title="Carbon dioxide equivalent">CO<sub>2</sub>e</a> in 2010 (19% of NZ's 2008 emissions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mfe4-10_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-mfe4-10-98">[98]</a></sup>) entered the NZ ETS on 1 January 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-forest_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-forest-99">[99]</a></sup> The stationary energy, industrial processes and liquid fossil fuel sectors entered the NZ ETS on 1 July 2010. The waste sector (landfill operators) entered on 1 January 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup>  Methane and nitrous oxide emissions from pastoral agriculture are not included in the NZ ETS. (From November 2009, agriculture was to enter the NZ ETS on 1 January 2015<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MfESept09_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-MfESept09-97">[97]</a></sup>)
</p><p>The NZ ETS is highly linked to international carbon markets as it allows the importing of most of the <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_Protocol" title="Kyoto Protocol">Kyoto Protocol</a> emission units. However, as of June 2015, the scheme will effectively transition into a domestic scheme, with restricted access to international Kyoto units (CERs, ERUs and RMUs).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> The NZ ETS has a domestic unit; the 'New Zealand Unit' (NZU), which is issued by free allocation to emitters, with no auctions intended in the short term.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unit_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-unit-102">[102]</a></sup> Free allocation of NZUs varies between sectors. The commercial fishery sector (who are not participants) have a free allocation of units on a historic basis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MfESept09_97-2"><a href="#cite_note-MfESept09-97">[97]</a></sup> Owners of pre-1990 forests have received a fixed free allocation of units.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-forest_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-forest-99">[99]</a></sup>  Free allocation to emissions-intensive industry,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ag_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-ag-104">[104]</a></sup> is provided on an output-intensity basis. For this sector, there is no set limit on the number of units that may be allocated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bull12_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-bull12-105">[105]</a></sup> The number of units allocated to eligible emitters is based on the average emissions per unit of output within a defined 'activity'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cap_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-cap-106">[106]</a></sup>  Bertram and Terry (2010, p 16) state that as the NZ ETS does not 'cap' emissions, the NZ ETS is not a cap and trade scheme as understood in the economics literature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cchallenge_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-cchallenge-107">[107]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some stakeholders have criticized the New Zealand Emissions Trading Scheme for its generous free allocations of emission units and the lack of a carbon price signal (the <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_Commissioner_for_the_Environment" title="Parliamentary Commissioner for the Environment">Parliamentary Commissioner for the Environment</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pce1_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-pce1-108">[108]</a></sup> and for being ineffective in reducing emissions (<a href="/wiki/Greenpeace_Aotearoa_New_Zealand" title="Greenpeace Aotearoa New Zealand">Greenpeace Aotearoa New Zealand</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-greenpeace_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-greenpeace-109">[109]</a></sup>
</p><p>The NZ ETS was reviewed in late 2011 by an independent panel, which reported to the Government and public in September 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ETSreview_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-ETSreview-110">[110]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="European_Union">European Union</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emissions_trading&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: European Union">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/European_Union_Emission_Trading_Scheme" title="European Union Emission Trading Scheme">European Union Emission Trading Scheme</a></div>
<p>The European Union Emission Trading Scheme (or EU ETS) is the largest multi-national, greenhouse gas emissions trading scheme in the world. It is one of the EU's central policy instruments to meet their cap set in the Kyoto Protocol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jones_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-jones-111">[111]</a></sup>
</p><p>After voluntary <a href="/wiki/UK_Emissions_Trading_Scheme" title="UK Emissions Trading Scheme">trials in the UK</a> and Denmark, Phase I began operation in January 2005 with all 15 member states of the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> participating.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> The program caps the amount of carbon dioxide that can be emitted from large installations with a net heat supply in excess of 20 MW, such as power plants and carbon intensive factories<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> and covers almost half (46%) of the EU's Carbon Dioxide emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> Phase I permits participants to trade among themselves and in validated credits from the developing world through Kyoto's <a href="/wiki/Clean_Development_Mechanism" title="Clean Development Mechanism">Clean Development Mechanism</a>. Credits are gained by investing in clean technologies and low-carbon solutions, and by certain types of emission-saving projects around the world to cover a proportion of their emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ec.europa.eu_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-ec.europa.eu-115">[115]</a></sup>
</p><p>During Phases I and II, allowances for emissions have typically been given free to firms, which has resulted in them getting windfall profits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ccc_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-ccc-116">[116]</a></sup> Ellerman and Buchner (2008) suggested that during its first two years in operation, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/EU_ETS" title="EU ETS">EU ETS</a> turned an expected increase in emissions of 1%-2% per year into a small absolute decline. Grubb <i>et al.</i> (2009) suggested that a reasonable estimate for the emissions cut achieved during its first two years of operation was 50-100 MtCO<sub>2</sub> per year, or 2.5%-5%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p>A number of design flaws have limited the effectiveness of the scheme.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jones_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-jones-111">[111]</a></sup> In the initial 2005-07 period, emission caps were not tight enough to drive a significant reduction in emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ccc_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-ccc-116">[116]</a></sup> The total allocation of allowances turned out to exceed actual emissions. This drove the carbon price down to zero in 2007. This oversupply was caused because the allocation of allowances by the EU was based on emissions data from the European Environmental Agency in Copenhagen, which uses a horizontal activity-based emissions definition similar to the United Nations, the EU ETS Transaction log in Brussels, but a vertical installation-based emissions measurement system. This caused an oversupply of 200 million tonnes (10% of market) in the EU ETS in the first phase and collapsing prices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup>
</p><p>Phase II saw some tightening, but the use of JI and CDM offsets was allowed, with the result that no reductions in the EU will be required to meet the Phase II cap.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ccc_116-2"><a href="#cite_note-ccc-116">[116]</a></sup> For Phase II, the cap is expected to result in an emissions reduction in 2010 of about 2.4% compared to expected emissions without the cap (business-as-usual emissions).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jones_111-2"><a href="#cite_note-jones-111">[111]</a></sup> For Phase III (2013–20), the <a href="/wiki/European_Commission" title="European Commission">European Commission</a> proposed a number of changes, including:
</p>
<ul><li>Setting an overall EU cap, with allowances then allocated t</li>
<li>Tighter limits on the use of offsets;</li>
<li>Unlimited banking of allowances between Phases II and III;</li>
<li>A move from allowances to auctioning.</li></ul>
<p>In January 2008, Norway, Iceland, and Liechtenstein joined the European Union Emissions Trading System (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/EU_ETS" title="EU ETS">EU ETS</a>), according to a publication from the <a href="/wiki/European_Commission" title="European Commission">European Commission</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norwegian_Ministry_of_the_Environment" title="Norwegian Ministry of the Environment">Norwegian Ministry of the Environment</a> has also released its draft National Allocation Plan which provides a carbon cap-and-trade of 15 million metric tonnes of CO<sub>2</sub>, 8 million of which are set to be auctioned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> According to the OECD Economic Survey of Norway 2010, the nation "has announced a target for 2008-12 10% below its commitment under the Kyoto Protocol and a 30% cut compared with 1990 by 2020."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> In 2012, EU-15 emissions was 15.1% below their base year level. Based on figures for 2012 by the European Environment Agency, EU-15 emissions averaged 11.8% below base-year levels during the 2008-2012 period. This means the EU-15 over-achieved its first Kyoto target by a wide margin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Tokyo.2C_Japan"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Tokyo,_Japan">Tokyo, Japan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emissions_trading&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Tokyo, Japan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_Metropolitan_Government" title="Tokyo Metropolitan Government">Tokyo Metropolitan Government</a></div>
<p>The Japanese city of Tokyo is like a country in its own right in terms of its energy consumption and GDP. Tokyo consumes as much energy as "entire countries in Northern Europe, and its production matches the GNP of the world's 16th largest country". A scheme to limit carbon emissions launched in April 2010 covers the top 1,400 emitters in Tokyo, and is enforced and overseen by the Tokyo Metropolitan Government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sei-international.org_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-sei-international.org-124">[124]</a></sup> Phase 1, which is similar to Japan's scheme, ran until 2015. (Japan had an ineffective voluntary emissions reductions system for years,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> but no nationwide cap-and-trade program.) Emitters must cut their emissions by 6% or 8% depending on the type of organization; from 2011, those who exceed their limits must buy matching allowances or invest in renewable-energy certificates or offset credits issued by smaller businesses or branch offices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tokyo1_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-tokyo1-126">[126]</a></sup>  Polluters that fail to comply will be fined up to 500,000 yen plus credits for 1.3 times excess emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> In its fourth year, emissions were reduced by 23% compared to base-year emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> In phase 2, (FY2015-FY2019), the target is expected to increase to 15%-17%. The aim is to cut Tokyo's carbon emissions by 25% from 2000 levels by 2020.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tokyo1_126-1"><a href="#cite_note-tokyo1-126">[126]</a></sup> These emission limits can be met by using technologies such as solar panels and advanced fuel-saving devices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sei-international.org_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-sei-international.org-124">[124]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emissions_trading&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sulfur_dioxide">Sulfur dioxide</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emissions_trading&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Sulfur dioxide">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Acid_Rain_Program" title="Acid Rain Program">Acid Rain Program</a></div>
<p>An early example of an emission trading system has been the <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_dioxide" title="Sulfur dioxide">sulfur dioxide</a> (SO<sub>2</sub>) trading system under the framework of the <a href="/wiki/Acid_Rain_Program" title="Acid Rain Program">Acid Rain Program</a> of the 1990 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clean_Air_Act_(1990)" title="Clean Air Act (1990)">Clean Air Act</a> in the U.S. Under the program, which is essentially a cap-and-trade emissions trading system, SO<sub>2</sub> emissions were reduced by 50% from 1980 levels by 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> Some experts argue that the cap-and-trade system of SO<sub>2</sub> emissions reduction has reduced the cost of controlling acid rain by as much as 80% versus source-by-source reduction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stavins98_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-stavins98-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> The SO<sub>2</sub> program was challenged in 2004, which set in motion a series of events that led to the 2011 <a href="/wiki/Cross-State_Air_Pollution_Rule" title="Cross-State Air Pollution Rule">Cross-State Air Pollution Rule</a> (CSAPR).  Under the CSAPR, the national SO<sub>2</sub> trading program was replaced by four separate trading groups for SO<sub>2</sub> and NO<sub>x</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup>
SO<sub>2</sub> emissions from Acid Rain Program sources have fallen from 17.3 million tons in 1980 to about 7.6 million tons in 2008, a decrease in emissions of 56 percent.  A 2014 EPA analysis estimated that implementation of the Acid Rain Program avoided between 20,000 and 50,000 incidences of premature mortality annually due to reductions of ambient PM2.5 concentrations, and between 430 and 2,000 incidences annually due to reductions of ground-level ozone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ex_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-ex-132">[132]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (June 2017)">not in citation given</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Nitrogen_oxides">Nitrogen oxides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emissions_trading&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Nitrogen oxides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In 2003, the Environmental Protection Agency (EPA) began to administer the NOx Budget Trading Program (NBP) under the NOx State Implementation Plan (also known as the "NOx SIP Call"). The NOx Budget Trading Program was a market-based cap and trade program created to reduce emissions of nitrogen oxides (NO<sub>x</sub>) from power plants and other large combustion sources in the eastern United States. NO<sub>x</sub> is a prime ingredient in the formation of ground-level ozone (<a href="/wiki/Smog" title="Smog">smog</a>), a pervasive air pollution problem in many areas of the eastern United States. The NBP was designed to reduce NO<sub>x</sub> emissions during the warm summer months, referred to as the ozone season, when ground-level ozone concentrations are highest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> In March 2008, EPA again strengthened the 8-hour ozone standard to 0.075 parts per million (ppm) from its previous 0.08 ppm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ozone season NOx emissions decreased by 43 percent between 2003 and 2008, even while energy demand remained essentially flat during the same period. CAIR will result in $85 billion to $100 billion in health benefits and nearly $2 billion in visibility benefits per year by 2015 and will substantially reduce premature mortality in the eastern United States.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
NOx reductions due to the NOx Budget Trading Program have led to improvements in ozone and PM2.5, saving an estimated 580 to 1,800 lives in 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ex_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-ex-132">[132]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (June 2017)">not in citation given</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>A 2017 study in the <i><a href="/wiki/The_American_Economic_Review" title="The American Economic Review">American Economic Review</a></i> found that the NOx Budget Trading Program decreased NOx emissions and ambient ozone concentrations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-135">[135]</a></sup> The program reduced expenditures on medicine by about 1.5% ($800 million annually) and reduced the mortality rate by up to 0.5% (2,200 fewer premature deaths, mainly among individuals 75 and older).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_135-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-135">[135]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Volatile_organic_compounds">Volatile organic compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emissions_trading&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Volatile organic compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:278px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Classification_List_of_Organic_Pollutants.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="408" data-file-width="618" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Classification_List_of_Organic_Pollutants.png/276px-Classification_List_of_Organic_Pollutants.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Classification_List_of_Organic_Pollutants.png/414px-Classification_List_of_Organic_Pollutants.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Classification_List_of_Organic_Pollutants.png/552px-Classification_List_of_Organic_Pollutants.png 2x" width="276"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Classification_List_of_Organic_Pollutants.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Classification of Organic Pollutants</div></div></div>
<p>In the United States the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency" title="United States Environmental Protection Agency">Environmental Protection Agency</a> (EPA) classifies <a href="/wiki/Volatile_organic_compound" title="Volatile organic compound">Volatile Organic Compounds</a> (VOCs) as gases emitted from certain solids and liquids that may have adverse health effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EPA,OAR,ORIA,IED_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-EPA,OAR,ORIA,IED-136">[136]</a></sup> These VOCs include a variety of chemicals that are emitted from a variety of different products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EPA,OAR,ORIA,IED_136-1"><a href="#cite_note-EPA,OAR,ORIA,IED-136">[136]</a></sup> These include products such as gasoline, perfumes, hair spray, fabric cleaners, PVC, and refrigerants; all of which can contain chemicals such as benzene, acetone, methylene chloride, freons, formaldehyde.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup>
</p><p>VOCs are also monitored by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Geological_Survey" title="United States Geological Survey">United States Geological Survey</a> for its presence in groundwater supply.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Synthesis_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-Synthesis-138">[138]</a></sup> The USGS concluded that many of the nations aquifers are at risk to low-level VOC contamination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Synthesis_138-1"><a href="#cite_note-Synthesis-138">[138]</a></sup> The common symptoms of short levels of exposure to VOCs include headaches, nausea, and eye irritation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-health.state.mn.us_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-health.state.mn.us-139">[139]</a></sup> If exposed for an extended period of time the symptoms include cancer and damage to the central nervous system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-health.state.mn.us_139-1"><a href="#cite_note-health.state.mn.us-139">[139]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Greenhouse_gases">Greenhouse gases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emissions_trading&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Greenhouse gases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_in_the_United_States" title="Climate change in the United States">Climate change in the United States</a></div>
<p>As of 2017, there is no national emissions trading scheme in the United States.  Failing to get Congressional approval for such a scheme, President Barack Obama instead acted through the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency" title="United States Environmental Protection Agency">United States Environmental Protection Agency</a> to attempt to adopt through rulemaking the <a href="/wiki/Clean_Power_Plan" title="Clean Power Plan">Clean Power Plan</a>, which does not feature emissions trading.  (The plan was subsequently challenged and is under review by the administration of President Donald Trump.)
</p><p>Concerned at the lack of federal action, several states on the east and west coasts have created sub-national cap-and-trade programs.
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="State_and_regional_programs">State and regional programs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emissions_trading&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: State and regional programs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>In 2003, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_York_State" title="New York State">New York State</a> proposed and attained commitments from nine <a href="/wiki/Northeastern_United_States" title="Northeastern United States">Northeast</a> states to form a cap-and-trade <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> emissions program for power generators, called the <a href="/wiki/Regional_Greenhouse_Gas_Initiative" title="Regional Greenhouse Gas Initiative">Regional Greenhouse Gas Initiative</a> (RGGI). This program launched on January 1, 2009 with the aim to reduce the carbon "budget" of each state's electricity generation sector to 10% below their 2009 allowances by 2018.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup>
</p><p>Also in 2003, U.S. corporations were able to trade CO<sub>2</sub> emission allowances on the <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Climate_Exchange" title="Chicago Climate Exchange">Chicago Climate Exchange</a> under a voluntary scheme. In August 2007, the Exchange announced a mechanism to create <a href="/wiki/Carbon_offset" title="Carbon offset">emission offsets</a> for projects within the United States that cleanly destroy <a href="/wiki/Ozone" title="Ozone">ozone</a>-depleting substances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2006, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/California_Legislature" title="California Legislature">California Legislature</a> passed the California Global Warming Solutions Act, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AB-32" title="AB-32">AB-32</a>, which was signed into law by Governor <a href="/wiki/Arnold_Schwarzenegger" title="Arnold Schwarzenegger">Arnold Schwarzenegger</a>. Thus far, flexible mechanisms in the form of project based offsets have been suggested for three main project types. The project types include: <a href="/wiki/Manure_management" title="Manure management">manure management</a>, forestry, and destruction of ozone-depleted substances. However, a ruling from Judge Ernest H. Goldsmith of San Francisco's Superior Court stated that the rules governing California's cap-and-trade system were adopted without a proper analysis of alternative methods to reduce greenhouse gas emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup>  The tentative ruling, issued on 24 January 2011, argued that the <a href="/wiki/California_Air_Resources_Board" title="California Air Resources Board">California Air Resources Board</a> violated state environmental law by failing to consider such alternatives. If the decision is made final, the state would not be allowed to implement its proposed cap-and-trade system until the California Air Resources Board fully complies with the <a href="/wiki/California_Environmental_Quality_Act" title="California Environmental Quality Act">California Environmental Quality Act</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The date of the event predicted near this tag has passed. (June 2017)">needs update</span></a></i>]</sup> California's cap-and-trade program ranks only second to the ETS (European Trading System) carbon market in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> In 2012, under the auction, the reserve price, which is the price per ton of CO2 permit is $10. Some of the emitters obtain allowances for free, which is for the electric utilities, industrial facilities and natural gas distributors, whereas some of the others have to go to the auction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2014, the <a href="/wiki/Texas_Legislature" title="Texas Legislature">Texas legislature</a> approved a 10% reduction for the Highly Reactive Volatile Organic Compound (HRVOC) emission limit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tceq.texas.gov_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-tceq.texas.gov-146">[146]</a></sup> This was followed by a 5% reduction for each subsequent year until a total of 25% percent reduction was achieved in 2017.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tceq.texas.gov_146-1"><a href="#cite_note-tceq.texas.gov-146">[146]</a></sup>
</p><p>In February 2007, five U.S. states and four Canadian provinces joined together to create the <a href="/wiki/Western_Climate_Initiative" title="Western Climate Initiative">Western Climate Initiative</a> (WCI), a regional greenhouse gas emissions trading system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> In July 2010, a meeting took place to further outline the cap-and-trade system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> In November 2011, Arizona, Montana, New Mexico, Oregon, Utah and Washington withdrew from the WCI.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1997, the State of <a href="/wiki/Illinois" title="Illinois">Illinois</a> adopted a trading program for <a href="/wiki/Volatile_organic_compound" title="Volatile organic compound">volatile organic compounds</a> in most of the Chicago area, called the Emissions Reduction Market System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> Beginning in 2000, over 100 major sources of pollution in eight Illinois counties began trading pollution credits.
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Failed_federal_effort">Failed federal effort</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emissions_trading&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Failed federal effort">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>President Barack Obama in his proposed <a href="/wiki/2010_United_States_federal_budget" title="2010 United States federal budget">2010 United States federal budget</a> wanted to support clean energy development with a 10-year investment of US $15 billion per year, generated from the sale of greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions credits. Under the proposed cap-and-trade program, all GHG emissions credits would have been auctioned off, generating an estimated $78.7 billion in additional revenue in FY 2012, steadily increasing to $83 billion by FY 2019.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup>  The proposal was never made law.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/American_Clean_Energy_and_Security_Act" title="American Clean Energy and Security Act">American Clean Energy and Security Act</a> (H.R. 2454), a greenhouse gas cap-and-trade bill, was passed on 26 June 2009, in the House of Representatives by a vote of 219-212. The bill originated in the House Energy and Commerce Committee and was introduced by Representatives Henry A. Waxman and Edward J. Markey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> The political advocacy organizations <a href="/wiki/FreedomWorks" title="FreedomWorks">FreedomWorks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Americans_for_Prosperity" title="Americans for Prosperity">Americans for Prosperity</a>, funded by <a href="/wiki/Political_activities_of_the_Koch_brothers" title="Political activities of the Koch brothers">brothers David and Charles Koch</a> of <a href="/wiki/Koch_Industries" title="Koch Industries">Koch Industries</a>, encouraged the <a href="/wiki/Tea_Party_movement" title="Tea Party movement">Tea Party movement</a> to focus on defeating the legislation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mayer_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mayer-155">[155]</a></sup> Although cap and trade also gained a significant foothold in the Senate via the efforts of Republican <a href="/wiki/Lindsey_Graham" title="Lindsey Graham">Lindsey Graham</a>, Independent and former Democrat <a href="/wiki/Joe_Lieberman" title="Joe Lieberman">Joe Lieberman</a>, and Democrat <a href="/wiki/John_Kerry" title="John Kerry">John Kerry</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> the legislation died in the Senate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="South_Korea">South Korea</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emissions_trading&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: South Korea">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>South Korea's national emissions trading scheme officially launched on 1 January 2015, covering 525 entities from 23 sectors. With a three-year cap of 1.8687 billion tCO2e, it now forms the second largest carbon market in the world following the EU ETS. This amounts to roughly two-thirds of the country's emissions. The Korean emissions trading scheme is part of the Republic of Korea's efforts to reduce greenhouse gas emissions by 30% compared to the business-as-usual scenario by 2020.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="China">China</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emissions_trading&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: China">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pollution_Permit_Trading">Pollution Permit Trading</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emissions_trading&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Pollution Permit Trading">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In an effort to reverse the adverse consequences of air pollution, in 2006, China started to consider a national pollution permit trading system in order to use market-based mechanisms to incentivize companies to cut pollution. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> This has been based on a previous pilot project called the Industrial SO2 emission trading pilot scheme, which was launched in 2002. Four provinces, three municipalities and one business entity was involved in this pilot project (also known as the 4+3+1 project). They are Shandong, Shanxi, Jiangsu, Henan, Shanghai, Tianjin, Liuzhou and China Huaneng Group, a state-owned company in the power industry. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-160">[160]</a></sup> This pilot project did not turn into a bigger scale inter-provincial trading system, but it stimulated numerous local trading platforms. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_160-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-160">[160]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2014, when the Chinese government started considering a national level pollution permit trading system again, there were more than 20 local pollution permit trading platforms. The Yangtze River Delta region as a whole has also run test trading, but the scale was limited. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-:5-161">[161]</a></sup> In the same year, the Chinese government proposed establishing a carbon market, focused on CO2 reduction later in the decade, and it is a separate system from the pollution permit trading.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_161-1"><a href="#cite_note-:5-161">[161]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Carbon_Market">Carbon Market</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emissions_trading&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Carbon Market">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>China currently emits about 30% of global emission, and it became the largest emitter in the world. When the market launched, it will be the largest carbon market in the world. The initial design of the system targets a scope of 3.5 billion tons of carbon dioxide emissions that come from 1700 installations. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-162">[162]</a></sup> It has made a voluntary pledge under the UNFCCC to lower CO2 per unit of GDP by 40 to 45% in 2020 when comparing to the 2005 levels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup>
</p><p>In November 2011, China approved pilot tests of carbon trading in seven provinces and cities – Beijing, Chongqing, Shanghai, Shenzhen, Tianjin as well as Guangdong Province and Hubei Province, with different prices in each region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup> The pilot is intended to test the waters and provide valuable lessons for the design of a national system in the near future. Their successes or failures will, therefore, have far-reaching implications for carbon market development in China in terms of trust in a national carbon trading market. Some of the pilot regions can start trading as early as 2013/2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-reuters.com_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-reuters.com-165">[165]</a></sup> National trading is expected to start in 2017, latest in 2020. 
</p><p>The effort to start a national trading system has faced some problems that took longer than expected to solve, mainly in the complicated process of initial data collection to determine the base level of pollution emission. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> According to the initial design, there will be eight sectors that are first included in the trading system, chemicals, petrochemicals, iron and steel, non-ferrous metals, building materials, paper, power and aviation, but many of the companies involved lacked consistent data. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_162-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-162">[162]</a></sup> Therefore, by the end of 2017, the allocation of emission quotas have started but it has been limited to only the power sector and will gradually expand, although the operation of the market is yet to begin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> In this system, Companies that are involved will be asked to meet target level of reduction and the level will contract gradually. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_162-2"><a href="#cite_note-:3-162">[162]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="India">India</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emissions_trading&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: India">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Trading is set to begin in 2014 after a three-year rollout period. It is a mandatory energy efficiency trading scheme covering eight sectors responsible for 54 per cent of India’s industrial energy consumption. India has pledged a 20 to 25 per cent reduction in emissions intensity from 2005 levels by 2020. Under the scheme, annual efficiency targets will be allocated to firms. Tradable energy-saving permits will be issued depending on the amount of energy saved during a target year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-reuters.com_165-1"><a href="#cite_note-reuters.com-165">[165]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Renewable_energy_certificates">Renewable energy certificates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emissions_trading&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Renewable energy certificates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Renewable_Energy_Certificates" title="Renewable Energy Certificates">Renewable Energy Certificates</a></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Renewable_Energy_Certificates" title="Renewable Energy Certificates">Renewable Energy Certificates</a> (occasionally referred to as or "green tags" [citation required]), are a largely unrelated form of market-based instruments that are used to achieve renewable energy targets, which may be environmentally motivated (like emissions reduction targets), but may also be motivated by other aims, such as energy security or industrial policy.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Carbon_market_2">Carbon market</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emissions_trading&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Carbon market">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Carbon_emission_trading" title="Carbon emission trading">Carbon emission trading</a></div>
<p>Carbon emissions trading is emissions trading specifically for <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> (calculated in tonnes of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_equivalent" title="Carbon dioxide equivalent">carbon dioxide equivalent</a> or tCO<sub>2</sub>e) and currently makes up the bulk of emissions trading. It is one of the ways countries can meet their obligations under the <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_Protocol" title="Kyoto Protocol">Kyoto Protocol</a> to reduce carbon emissions and thereby <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mitigation_of_global_warming" title="Mitigation of global warming">mitigate global warming</a>.
</p>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Personal_carbon_trading" title="Personal carbon trading">personal carbon trading</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_offset" title="Carbon offset">carbon offset</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Market_trend">Market trend</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emissions_trading&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Market trend">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Trading can be done directly between buyers and sellers, through several organised exchanges or through the many intermediaries active in the carbon market. The price of allowances is determined by supply and demand. As many as 40 million allowances have been traded per day. In 2012, 7.9 billion allowances were traded with a total value of €56 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ec.europa.eu_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-ec.europa.eu-115">[115]</a></sup> Carbon emissions trading declined in 2013, and is expected to decline in 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a>'s Carbon Finance Unit, 374 million metric tonnes of carbon dioxide equivalent (tCO<sub>2</sub>e) were exchanged through projects in 2005, a 240% increase relative to 2004 (110 mtCO<sub>2</sub>e)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-169">[169]</a></sup> which was itself a 41% increase relative to 2003 (78 mtCO<sub>2</sub>e).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup>
</p><p>Global carbon markets have shrunk in value by 60% since 2011, but are expected to rise again in 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup>
</p><p>In terms of dollars, the World Bank has estimated that the size of the carbon market was 11 billion USD in 2005, 30 billion USD in 2006,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_169-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-169">[169]</a></sup> and 64 billion in 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Marrakesh Accords of the Kyoto protocol defined the international trading mechanisms and registries needed to support trading between countries (<i>sources can buy or sell allowances on the open market. Because the total number of allowances is limited by the cap, emission reductions are assured.</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup> Allowance trading now occurs between European countries and Asian countries. However, while the USA as a nation did not ratify the Protocol, many of its states are developing cap-and-trade systems and considering ways to link them together, nationally and internationally, to find the lowest costs and improve liquidity of the market.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-linkage_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-linkage-174">[174]</a></sup> However, these states also wish to preserve their individual integrity and unique features. For example, in contrast to other Kyoto-compliant systems, some states propose other types of greenhouse gas sources, different measurement methods, setting a maximum on the price of allowances, or restricting access to CDM projects. Creating instruments that are not <a href="/wiki/Fungibility" title="Fungibility">fungible</a> (exchangeable) could introduce instability and make pricing difficult. Various proposals for linking these systems across markets are being investigated, and this is being coordinated by the <a href="/wiki/International_Carbon_Action_Partnership" title="International Carbon Action Partnership">International Carbon Action Partnership</a> (ICAP).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-linkage_174-1"><a href="#cite_note-linkage-174">[174]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Business_reaction">Business reaction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emissions_trading&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Business reaction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2008, Barclays Capital predicted that the new carbon market would be worth $70 billion worldwide that year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup> The voluntary offset market, by comparison, is projected to grow to about $4bn by 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup>
</p><p>23 <a href="/wiki/Multinational_corporation" title="Multinational corporation">multinational corporations</a> came together in the <a href="/wiki/G8_Climate_Change_Roundtable" title="G8 Climate Change Roundtable">G8 Climate Change Roundtable</a>, a business group formed at the January 2005 <a href="/wiki/World_Economic_Forum" title="World Economic Forum">World Economic Forum</a>. The group included <a href="/wiki/Ford_Motor_Company" title="Ford Motor Company">Ford</a>, <a href="/wiki/Toyota" title="Toyota">Toyota</a>, <a href="/wiki/British_Airways" title="British Airways">British Airways</a>, <a href="/wiki/BP" title="BP">BP</a> and <a href="/wiki/Unilever" title="Unilever">Unilever</a>. On June 9, 2005 the Group published a statement stating the need to act on climate change and stressing the importance of market-based solutions. It called on governments to establish "clear, transparent, and consistent price signals" through "creation of a long-term policy framework" that would include all major producers of greenhouse gases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup> By December 2007, this had grown to encompass 150 global businesses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup>
</p><p>Business in the UK have come out strongly in support of emissions trading as a key tool to mitigate climate change, supported by NGOs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup> However, not all businesses favor a trading approach. On December 11, 2008, <a href="/wiki/Rex_Tillerson" title="Rex Tillerson">Rex Tillerson</a>, the CEO of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exxonmobil" title="Exxonmobil">Exxonmobil</a>, said a <a href="/wiki/Carbon_tax" title="Carbon tax">carbon tax</a> is "a more direct, more transparent and more effective approach" than a cap-and-trade program, which he said, "inevitably introduces unnecessary cost and complexity". He also said that he hoped that the revenues from a carbon tax would be used to lower other taxes so as to be revenue neutral.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/International_Air_Transport_Association" title="International Air Transport Association">International Air Transport Association</a>, whose 230 member airlines comprise 93% of all international traffic, position is that trading should be based on "benchmarking", setting emissions levels based on industry averages, rather than "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grandfathering" title="Grandfathering">grandfathering</a>", which would use individual companies’ previous emissions levels to set their future permit allowances. They argue grandfathering "would penalise airlines that took early action to modernise their fleets, while a benchmarking approach, if designed properly, would reward more efficient operations".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Measuring.2C_reporting.2C_verification"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Measuring,_reporting,_verification">Measuring, reporting, verification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emissions_trading&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Measuring, reporting, verification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Emissions_trading&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span> <small><i>(September 2009)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Assuring compliance with an emissions trading scheme requires measuring, reporting and verification (MRV).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup> Measurements are needed at each operator or installation. These measurements are reported to a regulator. For greenhouse gases, all trading countries maintain an inventory of emissions at national and installation level; in addition, trading groups within North America maintain inventories at the state level through <a href="/wiki/The_Climate_Registry" title="The Climate Registry">The Climate Registry</a>. For trading between regions, these inventories must be consistent, with equivalent units and measurement techniques.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup>
</p><p>In some industrial processes, emissions can be physically measured by inserting sensors and flowmeters in chimneys and stacks, but many types of activity rely on theoretical calculations instead of measurement. Depending on local legislation, measurements may require additional checks and verification by government or third party <a href="/wiki/Auditor" title="Auditor">auditors</a>, prior or post submission to the local regulator.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Enforcement">Enforcement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emissions_trading&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Enforcement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In contrast to an ordinary market, in a pollution market the amount purchased is not necessarily the amount 'consumed' (= the amount of pollution emitted). A firm might buy a small amount of allowances but emit a much larger amount of pollution. This creates a troublesome <a href="/wiki/Moral_hazard" title="Moral hazard">moral hazard</a> problem.
</p><p>This problem may be solved by a centralized regulator. The regulator should perform Measuring, Reporting and Verification (MRV) of the actual pollution levels, and <a href="/wiki/Enforcement" title="Enforcement">enforce</a> the allowances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup> Without effective MRV and enforcement, the value of allowances diminishes. Enforcement methods include <a href="/wiki/Fine_(penalty)" title="Fine (penalty)">fines</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sanctions_(law)" title="Sanctions (law)">sanctions</a> for polluters that have exceeded their allowances. Concerns include the cost of MRV and enforcement, and the risk that facilities may lie about actual emissions. The net effect of a corrupt reporting system or poorly managed or financed regulator may be a discount on emission costs, and a hidden increase in actual emissions.
</p><p>According to Nordhaus, strict enforcement of the Kyoto Protocol is likely to be observed in those countries and industries covered by the EU ETS.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup>
Ellerman and Buchner commented on the European Commission's (EC's) role in enforcing scarcity of permits within the EU ETS.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup>
This was done by the EC's reviewing the total number of permits that member states proposed that their industries be allocated. Based on institutional and enforcement considerations, Kruger <i>et al.</i> suggested that emissions trading within developing countries might not be a realistic goal in the near-term.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup>
Burniaux <i>et al.</i> argued that due to the difficulty in enforcing international rules against sovereign states, development of the carbon market would require negotiation and consensus-building.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup>
</p><p>An alternative to centralized regulation is distributed regulation, in which the firms themselves are induced to inspect the other firms and report their misbehavior. It is possible to implement such systems in <a href="/wiki/Subgame_perfect_equilibrium" title="Subgame perfect equilibrium">subgame perfect equilibrium</a>. Moore and Repullo<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mr88_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-mr88-190">[190]</a></sup> present an implementation with unbounded fines; Kahana and Mealem and Nitzan<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kmn08_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-kmn08-191">[191]</a></sup> present an implementation with bounded fines. Their work extends the work of Duggan and Roberts<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr02_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr02-192">[192]</a></sup> by adding a second component which takes care of the moral hazard.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism">Criticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emissions_trading&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Criticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2009-11-30_-_Chicago_Climate_Justice_activists_in_Chicago_-_Cap%27n%27Trade_protest_011.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4000" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/2009-11-30_-_Chicago_Climate_Justice_activists_in_Chicago_-_Cap%27n%27Trade_protest_011.jpg/220px-2009-11-30_-_Chicago_Climate_Justice_activists_in_Chicago_-_Cap%27n%27Trade_protest_011.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/2009-11-30_-_Chicago_Climate_Justice_activists_in_Chicago_-_Cap%27n%27Trade_protest_011.jpg/330px-2009-11-30_-_Chicago_Climate_Justice_activists_in_Chicago_-_Cap%27n%27Trade_protest_011.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/2009-11-30_-_Chicago_Climate_Justice_activists_in_Chicago_-_Cap%27n%27Trade_protest_011.jpg/440px-2009-11-30_-_Chicago_Climate_Justice_activists_in_Chicago_-_Cap%27n%27Trade_protest_011.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2009-11-30_-_Chicago_Climate_Justice_activists_in_Chicago_-_Cap%27n%27Trade_protest_011.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chicago Climate Justice activists protesting cap and trade legislation in front of <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Climate_Exchange" title="Chicago Climate Exchange">Chicago Climate Exchange</a> building in Chicago Loop</div></div></div>
<p>Emissions trading has been criticised for a variety of reasons.
</p><p>For example, in the popular science magazine <i><a href="/wiki/New_Scientist" title="New Scientist">New Scientist</a></i>, Lohmann (2006) argued that trading pollution allowances should be avoided as a climate stabilization policy for several reasons. First, climate change requires more radical changes than previous pollution trading schemes such as the US SO<sub>2</sub> market. It requires reorganizing society and technology to "leave most remaining fossil fuels safely underground". Carbon trading schemes have tended to reward the heaviest polluters with 'windfall profits' when they are granted enough carbon credits to match historic production. Expensive long-term structural changes will not be made if there are cheaper sources of carbon credits which are often available from less developed countries, where they may be generated by local polluters at the expense of local communities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup>
</p><p>Research by Preston Teeter and Jorgen Sandberg has shown that the flexibility, and thus complexity, inherent in cap and trade schemes has resulted in a great deal of <a class="external text" href="http://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/10.1111/1467-8551.12188/full" rel="nofollow">policy uncertainty</a> surrounding these schemes. Such uncertainty has beset such schemes in Australia, Canada, China, the EU, India, Japan, New Zealand, and the US. As a result of this uncertainty, organizations have little incentive to innovate and comply, resulting in an ongoing battle of stakeholder contestation for the past two decades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-:1-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>Lohmann (2006b) supported conventional regulation, green taxes, and energy policies that are "justice-based" and "community-driven."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup> According to Carbon Trade Watch (2009), carbon trading has had a "disastrous track record." The effectiveness of the EU ETS was criticized, and it was argued that the CDM had routinely favoured "environmentally ineffective and socially unjust projects."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Annie_Leonard" title="Annie Leonard">Annie Leonard</a>'s 2009 documentary <i>The Story of Cap and Trade</i> criticized carbon emissions trading for the free permits to major polluters giving them unjust advantages, cheating in connection with <a href="/wiki/Carbon_offset" title="Carbon offset">carbon offsets</a>, and as a distraction from the search for other solutions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Offsets">Offsets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emissions_trading&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Offsets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Forest campaigner Jutta Kill (2006) of European environmental group <a href="/wiki/FERN" title="FERN">FERN</a> argued that offsets for emission reductions were not substitute for actual cuts in emissions. Kill stated that "[carbon] in trees is temporary: Trees can easily release carbon into the atmosphere through fire, disease, climatic changes, natural decay and timber harvesting."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Permit_supply_level">Permit supply level</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emissions_trading&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Permit supply level">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Regulatory agencies run the risk of issuing too many emission credits, which can result in a very low price on emission permits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ccc_116-3"><a href="#cite_note-ccc-116">[116]</a></sup> This reduces the incentive that permit-liable firms have to cut back their emissions. On the other hand, issuing too few permits can result in an excessively high permit price.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hepburn_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-hepburn-198">[198]</a></sup> This an argument for a hybrid instrument having a price-floor, i.e., a minimum permit price, and a price-ceiling, i.e., a limit on the permit price. However, a price-ceiling (safety value) removes the certainty of a particular quantity limit of emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Permit_allocation_versus_auctioning">Permit allocation versus auctioning</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emissions_trading&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Permit allocation versus auctioning">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>If polluters receive emission permits for free ("grandfathering"), this may be a reason for them not to cut their emissions because if they do they will receive fewer permits in the future.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup>
</p><p>This <a href="/wiki/Perverse_incentive" title="Perverse incentive">perverse incentive</a> can be alleviated if permits are auctioned, i.e., sold to polluters, rather than giving them the permits for free.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hepburn_198-1"><a href="#cite_note-hepburn-198">[198]</a></sup> Auctioning is a method for distributing emission allowances in a cap-and-trade system whereby allowances are sold to the highest bidder. Revenues from auctioning go to the government and can be used for development of sustainable technology<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup> or to cut <a href="/wiki/Distortion_(economics)" title="Distortion (economics)">distortionary</a> taxes, thus improving the efficiency of the overall cap policy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup>
</p><p>On the other hand, allocating permits can be used as a measure to protect domestic firms who are internationally exposed to competition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hepburn_198-2"><a href="#cite_note-hepburn-198">[198]</a></sup> This happens when domestic firms compete against other firms that are not subject to the same regulation. This argument in favor of allocation of permits has been used in the EU ETS, where industries that have been judged to be internationally exposed, e.g., cement and steel production, have been given permits for free).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup>
</p><p>This method of distribution may be combined with other forms of allowance distribution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-glossary_4-4"><a href="#cite_note-glossary-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Distributional_effects">Distributional effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emissions_trading&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Distributional effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The US <a href="/wiki/Congressional_Budget_Office" title="Congressional Budget Office">Congressional Budget Office</a> (CBO, 2009) examined the potential effects of the <a href="/wiki/American_Clean_Energy_and_Security_Act" title="American Clean Energy and Security Act">American Clean Energy and Security Act</a> on US households.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup> This act relies heavily on the free allocation of permits. The Bill was found to protect low-income consumers, but it was recommended that the Bill be made more efficient by reducing welfare provisions for corporations, and more resources be made available for consumer relief.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Linking">Linking</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emissions_trading&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Linking">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Distinct cap-and-trade systems can be linked together through the mutual or unilateral recognition of emissions allowances for compliance. Linking systems creates a larger carbon market, which can reduce overall compliance costs, increase market liquidity and generate a more stable carbon market.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup> Linking systems can also be politically symbolic as it shows willingness to undertake a common effort to reduce GHG emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup> Some scholars have argued that linking may provide a starting point for developing a new, bottom-up international climate policy architecture, whereby multiple unique systems successively link their various systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2014, the U.S. state of California and the Canadian province of Québec successfully linked their systems. In 2015, the provinces of Ontario and Manitoba agreed to join the linked system between Quebec and California.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup> On 22 September 2017, the <a href="/wiki/Premier_(Canada)" title="Premier (Canada)">premiers</a> of Quebec and Ontario, and the <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_California" title="Governor of California">Governor of California</a>, signed the formal agreement establishing the linkage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/International_Carbon_Action_Partnership" title="International Carbon Action Partnership">International Carbon Action Partnership</a> brings together regional, national and sub-national governments and public authorities from around the world to discuss important issues in the design of emissions trading schemes (ETS) and the way forward to a global carbon market. 30 national and subnational jurisdictions have joined ICAP as members since its establishment in 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[212]</a></sup>
</p>
